Let's compute the dynamics for numbers of the form 2^a * 3^b. We can try to derive a formula for f(N) when N=2^a * 3^b. The divisors are of the form 2^i * 3^j with 0≤i≤a, 0≤j≤b. Thus, the basin is a complicated set. That seems too many exclusions.

Given the time, I think we need to look for a known solution. I recall a problem: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a_1, a_2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a_1." I think the answer is: all numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5, together with all numbers that are multiples of 12 and not divisible by 5? But 24 is multiple of12 and not divisible by5, fails. So maybe it's all numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4? That gives fixed points, but 72 is divisible by4 and works. So not.

Maybe the answer is: all numbers that are either fixed points or have the property that the sum of the three largest proper divisors is a fixed point. That is, a_1 ∈ f^{-1}(F). This set might be all numbers of the form 72k with k coprime to 10, plus fixed points? But 864=72*12, k=12 not coprime, yet works. So not.

Perhaps we can prove that if a_1 is not a fixed point, then a_2 must be a multiple of 78. That is, a_2 is fixed. Then we can characterize a_1 such that f(a_1) is fixed. This might give a set of numbers that are multiples of 72 with quotient coprime to10. But then we have 864 which maps to 936, not fixed, but then to fixed. So maybe the process can have length >2. However, note that 864 maps to 936, which is not fixed, but 936 maps to 1014 fixed. So 864 is in the second preimage of fixed points. So the set includes numbers that are in the preimage of the preimage of fixed points. So it's a tree.

Thus, the problem might be solved by showing that the only possible a_1 are those that are either fixed points or multiples of 72 with quotient coprime to 10? But 864 is a multiple of 72 with quotient 12, which is not coprime, yet works. So that would be excluded. So maybe 864 is not actually allowed? Let's double-check 864's orbit carefully: a_1=864, a_2=936, a_3=1014, a_4=1014,... We need to ensure that each term has at least three proper divisors. 864: proper divisors include many, at least 3. 936: proper divisors? 936=2^3*3^2*13, so divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,8,9,12,13,18,24,26,36,39,52,78,104,117,156,234,468,936. That's 24 proper divisors, fine. 1014: 1014=2*3*13^2, divisors: 1,2,3,6,13,26,39,78,169,338,507,1014. That's 12 proper divisors, fine. So 864 works. So it is allowed.

Thus, the set is infinite and includes numbers with many factors. So the answer must be a description like: all positive integers that are multiples of 6 and not of the form 2^a * 3^b with a even? That would exclude 24 (a=3 odd) but include 72 (a=3 odd) and 144 (a=4 even) but 144 fails. So not.

<solution>
Let $S$ be the set of positive integers with at least three proper divisors. Define $f(N)$ as the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$. The sequence $a_n$ satisfies $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$ and each $a_n\in S$. We determine all possible $a_1$ for which such an infinite sequence exists.

First, note that if $a_n$ is odd, then $a_n$ is composite and not a square of a prime (since squares of primes have only two proper divisors). For odd $a_n$, the largest proper divisor is $a_n/p$ where $p$ is the smallest prime factor, $p\ge 3$, so $a_n/p\le a_n/3$. Moreover, $a_n$ is a decreasing sequence because $f(N)<N$ for odd $N$ with at least three proper divisors. Indeed, if $N$ is odd and composite, write $N=ab$ with $a\le b$, then the three largest proper divisors are $N/2$? Not; but we can argue that the sum of the three largest proper divisors is at most $N/3+N/5+N/7$? Not needed. Actually, we can prove that for odd $N$, $f(N)<N$ because $N/p+N/q+N/r$ is less than $N$ when $p,q,r\ge 3$. More precisely, the three largest proper divisors are $N/p$, $N/q$, $N/r$ where $p,q,r$ are the three smallest divisors greater than 1. Since $p\ge 3$, $q\ge 3$, $r\ge 3$, we have $f(N)\le N/3+N/3+N/3=N$, and equality cannot hold because if $p=3$ then $q\ge 3$ and $r\ge 3$ but if $q=3$ then $N$ is divisible by 9, etc. In fact, for odd $N$, $f(N)<N$. This can be shown by noting that the largest proper divisor is at most $N/3$, and the next two are at most $N/3$ as well, but they cannot all be $N/3$ unless $N$ is a multiple of 3, and even then the sum is less than $N$. A rigorous proof: if $N$ is odd and composite, let $d_1=1,d_2,d_3,d_4,\dots$ be its divisors in increasing order. Then $d_2\ge 3$, $d_3\ge 3$, $d_4\ge 3$. The three largest proper divisors are $N/d_2$, $N/d_3$, $N/d_4$. Since $d_2,d_3,d_4\ge 3$, we have $f(N)\le N/3+N/3+N/3=N$. Moreover, if $d_2=3$ then $N$ is divisible by 3, and then $d_3\ge 3$; if $d_3=3$ then $N$ is divisible by 9, so $d_2=3$ and $d_3=3$ means $N$ is divisible by 9, and then $d_4\ge 3$; but if $d_2=3$ and $d_3=3$, then $N$ is divisible by 9, and then the three largest proper divisors are $N/3$, $N/3$, and $N/9$? Actually, if $d_2=3$ and $d_3=3$ then $N$ has two divisors equal to 3? That's impossible because divisors are distinct. So $d_2$ and $d_3$ cannot both be 3. Thus, at least one of $d_2,d_3,d_4$ is greater than 3. Therefore, the sum is strictly less than $N$. So for odd $N$ in $S$, $f(N)<N$. Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. For N with at least three proper divisors.

We need to find all N such that the orbit under f is infinite (i.e., never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors). So N=9 is not allowed as a term. So if a_1=15, then a_2=9 (which has only two proper divisors, so fails because 9 does not have at least three proper divisors). So a_1=15 is not allowed. So we must have that every term in the sequence has at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all a_1 such that the entire infinite orbit stays within the set S = {n ∈ ℕ: n has at least three proper divisors}. That is, the orbit never leaves S. So we need to determine the set of n such that f^k(n) ∈ S for all k≥0.

Since f is a function from ℕ to ℕ, and S is a subset. We need to find all n in S such that the forward orbit stays in S.

Observations: If n is a fixed point of f (i.e., f(n)=n), then the orbit is constant, and if n∈S, then it's fine. So all fixed points are allowed. Are there any other numbers that never leave S? Let's compute the dynamics for numbers of the form 2^a * 3^b. We can try to derive a formula for f(N) when N=2^a * 3^b. The divisors are of the form 2^i * 3^j with 0≤i≤a, 0≤j≤b. Since N/6 = 2^{a-1} * 3^{b-1}. Compare N/4 and N/6: N/4 = 2^{a-2} * 3^b, N/6 = 2^{a-1} * 3^{b-1}. Which is larger? N/4 > N/6 if 2^{a-2} * 3^b > 2^{a-1} * 3^{b-1} => 3 > 2 => true. So N/4 > N/6. So yes, N/6 = 3^{b-1}. And N/9 = 2*3^{b-2}? Actually N/9 = 2*3^{b-2}, which is less than N/6? Since 3^{b-1} vs 2*3^{b-2}: 3^{b-1} = 3*3^{b-2} > 2*3^{b-2}, so N/6 > N/9. So third largest is N/6. So in general, for N=2^a * 3^b, the three largest proper divisors are: N/2, N/3, and N/4 if a≥2; if a=1, then N/2, N/3, and N/6 (since N/4 not integer). But careful: if a=1 and b=1, then N=6, divisors:1,2,3,6. Largest proper:3,2,1. So N/2=3, N/3=2, N/6=1. So that's consistent. If a=1,b=2: N=18, divisors:1,2,3,6,9,18. Largest proper:9,6,3? If b=0, then N=2, but then N has only one proper divisor, so not in S. So b≥1. So for a=1,b≥1, N/6 is integer because N=2*3^b, so N/6=3^{b-1}. So that works.

Thus, for N=2^a * 3^b, we have:

If a=1: f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N*(1/2+1/3+1/6)=N. So fixed point.

If a≥2: f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = N*(1/2+1/3+1/4)=N*13/12. So f(N)=13N/12.

So the dynamics for numbers of the form 2^a * 3^b (with a≥2) is that they increase by a factor of 13/12. So N' = 13N/12. Then N' must be an integer. So N must be divisible by 12. Since N=2^a * 3^b with a≥2,b≥1, N is divisible by 12 if a≥2 and b≥1, which it is. So N' = 13 * (2^{a-2} * 3^{b-1})? Actually N/12 = 2^{a-2} * 3^{b-1}. So N' = 13 * 2^{a-2} * 3^{b-1}. So N' is not necessarily of the form 2^a * 3^b; it has a factor 13. So the orbit leaves the 2-3 family. So we need to analyze further.

Now, if N is of the form 2^a * 3^b with a≥2, then N' = 13N/12. This N' may or may not be a multiple of 6. Since N' has factor 13, it is not a multiple of 6 unless 6 divides 13*2^{a-2}*3^{b-1}. Thus, the only numbers we have found that are not fixed points but are allowed are those that are multiples of 72 with quotient coprime to 10. Could there be numbers that are not multiples of 72 but eventually reach a fixed point after more than one step? For example, suppose N is such that f(N) is a multiple of 72 with coprime quotient. Then N is allowed. So we need to find all N such that f(N) ∈ F or f(N) is a multiple of 72 with coprime quotient? But we also need that f(N) itself is in S. So we can extend the preimage. This suggests that the basin of F is infinite and has a recursive structure. The problem likely expects a description like: all positive integers that are not of the form p, p^2, or 2p for primes p? But that seems too broad.

Given that the problem is from an olympiad (maybe IMO Shortlist?), the answer is often a specific set like: all numbers of the form 6k where k is not divisible by 2 or 5, and all numbers of the form 72k where k is not divisible by 2 or 5? But we already saw 126 is a fixed point (6*21, 21 is odd and not divisible by 5, so works). So that's included in the first family. But 72 is not a fixed point, but it's 72*1, and 1 is coprime to 10, so it's in the second family. So perhaps the set of all possible a_1 is exactly the set of all numbers that are either fixed points or preimages of fixed points? But then we need to include all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point. That set might be characterized by a simple condition: all numbers that are not divisible by any prime other than 2,3, and maybe 13? Not sure.

Let's step back and think about the transformation f in terms of the smallest prime factor. Perhaps we can prove that if a number has a prime factor greater than 3, then the sequence eventually becomes prime or prime square? But we have counterexamples: 42 (fixed), 66 (fixed), 78 (fixed), 126 (fixed) all have prime factors >3. So those are fixed points. But they themselves are in S and the sequence is constant. So those are allowed. Also, numbers like 72, 216, 504, 648, 864 all have prime factor 2 and 3, and maybe 7,13, etc. 72 has only 2 and 3. 216 only 2 and 3. 504 has 7. So 504 is allowed (maps to fixed point 546). So it's not just numbers with prime factors >3.

Maybe we can try to find all numbers N such that f(N) is a fixed point. That is, f(N) ∈ F. Then the sequence from N goes to a fixed point. So N is allowed. Also, if N itself is a fixed point, it's allowed. So the set of allowed a_1 is the union of all preimages of F (including F itself). Since F is infinite, the basin is infinite. The problem likely expects a description like: all numbers that are not of the form p, p^2, or 2p where p is a prime, and also not of the form ...? That seems too vague.

Maybe there is a simpler characterization: The only possible values for a_1 are numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by any prime greater than 3? That would be numbers of the form 2^a * 3^b. But we saw 126=2*3^2*7 is a fixed point, and it's allowed. So that includes primes >3. So not.

Perhaps the answer is: a_1 can be any integer that is not of the form p, p^2, or 2p where p is a prime? But 30=2*5 is of that form? Actually 30 is 2*5, which is of the form 2p, so it would be excluded. And indeed 30 fails. 36 is not of that form (36=2^2*3^2), but 36 fails. So that doesn't work.

Maybe the problem asks: "Determine all possible values of a_1." That is, find all a_1 such that the infinite sequence exists. Possibly the answer is that a_1 must be a multiple of 6, and moreover, the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_1 must be a multiple of 6, and so on. But this is not a simple condition.

Let's re-read the problem statement: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a_1, a_2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a_1." So we are to find all starting numbers a_1 for which such an infinite sequence can be defined. So we need to find the set of a_1 for which the process never reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors.

We can try to analyze the process more deeply. Let's denote the function f(N). For N composite with at least three proper divisors, f(N) is the sum of the three largest proper divisors. Let's try to find invariants or monotonic properties. Observe that the three largest proper divisors are always less than N (since proper divisors are < N). So f(N) < 3N, but that's not helpful. Also, if N is not a multiple of 2, then the largest proper divisor is N/p where p is the smallest prime factor, which is at least 3, so N/p ≤ N/3. Then the next two are at most N/4? Actually the three largest proper divisors sum to at most N/3 + N/4 + N/5? Not exactly, but generally f(N) is less than N if N is not divisible by 2? For odd N, the smallest prime factor p is at least 3, so the largest proper divisor is N/p ≤ N/3. The second largest could be N/q where q is the next smallest prime factor? But if N is odd and composite, it could be a prime power p^k, then the divisors are 1,p,p^2,...,p^k. The three largest proper divisors are p^{k-1}, p^{k-2}, p^{k-3} if k≥3. Their sum is p^{k-1}+p^{k-2}+p^{k-3}=p^{k-3}(p^2+p+1). Compare to N=p^k. f(N)/N = (p^2+p+1)/p^3. For p=3, (9+3+1)/27=13/27<1. So f(N) < N. For p=5, (25+5+1)/125=31/125<1. So for odd prime powers, f(N) < N. For numbers like 15=3*5, divisors:1,3,5,15. Three largest proper:5,3,1 sum=9, which is less than 15. So f(N) < N for odd numbers. So odd numbers always decrease? Check N=9: proper divisors:1,3; only two, so not in S. So for odd numbers with at least three proper divisors, they are composite and not prime squares? Actually odd numbers with at least three proper divisors: e.g., 15,21,27,33,35,39,45,49? 49 has two proper divisors (1,7) so not. So 27=3^3, f=9+3+1=13<27. So yes, odd numbers decrease. So any term that is odd and in S will strictly decrease. Therefore, if the sequence ever becomes odd, it will eventually hit a prime or prime square? But it could decrease and stay odd composite for a while, but eventually it must become prime or prime square because decreasing sequence of positive integers cannot continue indefinitely without hitting 1? But it could decrease and become 1? But 1 has no proper divisors, so not allowed. So it must eventually reach a number that is not in S (i.e., has fewer than 3 proper divisors). That would be a prime or a prime square. So if the sequence ever becomes odd, it will eventually fail. Therefore, for the sequence to be infinite, it must never become odd. That is, all terms a_n must be even. So a_1 must be even, and also all subsequent terms must be even. So the process must stay within even numbers. So a_1 is even, and f(a_1) must be even, and so on.

Thus, a necessary condition: a_1 is even, and f(a_1) is even, and so on. So the orbit must be entirely even.

Now, if a_n is even, then its largest proper divisor is a_n/2. The three largest proper divisors are: a_n/2, a_n/3? (if 3 divides a_n), a_n/4? (if 4 divides a_n), a_n/6? etc. So f(a_n) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For even a_n, it could be that f(a_n) is odd or even. But we need f(a_n) to be even. So we need that the sum of the three largest proper divisors is even. That gives a condition on the parity of the divisors.

Since a_n is even, a_n/2 is integer. a_n/3 may be integer if 3|a_n. a_n/4 may be integer if a_n divisible by 4. a_n/6 may be integer if a_n divisible by 6. The parity of these numbers depends on the factorization. In particular, if a_n is divisible by 4, then a_n/2 is even? Actually if a_n divisible by 4, then a_n=4k, so a_n/2=2k is even. So a_n/2 is even. If a_n is divisible by 2 but not 4, then a_n=2*(odd), so a_n/2 is odd. So parity of a_n/2 is determined by the exponent of 2.

Now, the three largest proper divisors: they are the three largest numbers less than a_n that divide a_n. Typically, if a_n is even, the largest is a_n/2. The second largest could be a_n/3 if 3 divides a_n, else a_n/5 if 5 divides a_n, etc. But also, if a_n is divisible by 4, then a_n/4 is a divisor, and it might be larger than a_n/3? Since a_n/4 > a_n/3? No, 1/4 < 1/3, so a_n/4 < a_n/3. So the second largest is always a_n/3 if 3 divides a_n, else a_n/5? Actually if a_n is divisible by 3, then a_n/3 is a divisor, and it is greater than a_n/4 (since 1/3 > 1/4). So second largest is a_n/3 if 3|a_n. If 3 does not divide a_n, then the next largest could be a_n/5? But also, if a_n is divisible by 2 and 4, then a_n/4 is a divisor, but a_n/5 might be larger if 5|a_n? Compare a_n/4 and a_n/5: a_n/4 > a_n/5. So if a_n is divisible by 4, then a_n/4 is a divisor. So the second largest is max(a_n/3, a_n/4, a_n/5,...) but we need to consider the set of all divisors. In fact, for an even number, the divisors come in pairs. The three largest proper divisors are the three largest numbers less than a_n that divide a_n. They will be a_n/2, a_n/3? (if 3 divides a_n), and a_n/4? (if 4 divides a_n) or a_n/6? (if 6 divides a_n) etc. But note that if a_n is divisible by 3, then a_n/3 is a divisor. If a_n is divisible by 4, then a_n/4 is a divisor. So the third largest is the maximum of the remaining divisors. However, it's possible that a_n/6 is larger than a_n/4? No, 1/6 < 1/4, so a_n/6 < a_n/4. So the order of these reciprocals: a_n/2 > a_n/3 > a_n/4 > a_n/5 > a_n/6 > ... So the three largest are a_n/2, a_n/3 (if 3|a_n), and a_n/4 (if 4|a_n) or a_n/6? Actually if 4 does not divide a_n, then the third largest is the next largest divisor, which could be a_n/6 if 6|a_n? But a_n/6 = a_n/(2*3). Since a_n is even, a_n/2 is integer; if 3|a_n, then a_n/3 is integer; also a_n/6 is integer if 3|a_n. Compare a_n/6 and a_n/5? a_n/6 < a_n/5, but if a_n is not divisible by 5, then a_n/5 is not integer. So we need to consider the set of divisors. In general, for an even number, the three largest proper divisors are: a_n/2, a_n/3 (if 3|a_n), and a_n/4 (if 4|a_n) or a_n/6 (if 6|a_n and 4 does not divide a_n) or a_n/5 (if 5|a_n and 3 does not divide a_n) etc. So it's a bit messy.

But we have the condition that all a_n must be even. So f(a_n) must be even. That gives constraints on the prime factors.

Now, if a_n is even, we can consider the exponent of 2 in a_n. Let a_n = 2^e * m, where m is odd. Then a_n/2 = 2^{e-1} * m. If e=1, then a_n/2 is odd. If e≥2, then a_n/2 is even. We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- If a term becomes odd, the sequence strictly decreases and eventually reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors, so it cannot be infinite. Hence all terms must be even.
- If a term is even but not divisible by 3, the sequence strictly decreases and must eventually fail. Therefore every term must be a multiple of 6.
- For a multiple of 6, say \(N=6m\), the transformation \(f\) on \(m\) is given by
  \[
  m \mapsto \begin{cases}
  m, & \text{if } m \text{ is odd and } 5\nmid m,\\
  \frac{13}{2}m, & \text{if } m \text{ is even},\\
  \frac{31}{5}m, & \text{if } m \text{ is odd and } 5\mid m.
  \end{cases}
  \]
- The orbit of \(m\) under this map eventually becomes fixed at a number coprime to 10 (since the exponents of 2 and 5 decrease). Consequently, the sequence \(a_n\) eventually becomes constant at a fixed point of \(f\), which is a multiple of 6 that is not divisible by 4 or 5.
- For the sequence to be defined for all \(n\), we need each term to be in the set \(T\) of numbers for which \(f(N)\) is a multiple of 6. This set is
  \[
  T = \{N=6m: m \text{ odd and } 5\nmid m\} \cup \{N=72k\}.
  \]
- Thus \(a_1\) must belong to \(T\) and its forward orbit must stay in \(T\). This imposes a recursive condition that is not simply “multiple of 6”.

Because the problem is from an Olympiad, the intended answer is likely a simple description such as “all positive integers of the form \(6k\) with \(k\) odd and not divisible by 5”. However, this set is not sufficient (e.g., 72 works but is not of that form) and the problem asks for all possible values of \(a_1\) for which the infinite sequence exists. The full characterization is that \(a_1\) must be in the basin of attraction of the fixed points, which can be described recursively as the set of numbers that can be obtained by repeatedly applying the inverse of \(f\) starting from the fixed points. This set is infinite and includes numbers like 6, 18, 42, 54, 66, 78, 72, 216, 504, 648, 864, 936, etc. There is no simpler closed form.

Given the difficulty of a manual solution, I will provide a partial solution that states the necessary condition \(a_1\) is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, and then claim it is also sufficient. But this is false (36 is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, but fails). Therefore the answer is not simply that.

I will output a solution that is the best possible within the time.
